Some of their friends sent their children there long before I was born.  Pastor Jones might have had Dorian Gray in mind when he said:  \\"Dissipations of youth must be paid for with a draft on old age.\\"<br /><br />Wilde's Dorian Gray is devoted to the fanciful premise that a soul might scar itself with the most horrible of depraved debauchery, which in Victorian London included opium dens, prostitution, and in the case of Mr. Gray, murder, both by proxy and in personal excess of temper.  So, if you exercise patience, a murder mystery unfolds. (Lots and lots of patience!)<br /><br />What dark meditations crossed my mind, and how like ice was my countenance while feasting in Wilde's psychological eulogizing of scarlet sins and opulence from dozens of characters famous in classical Western history.  As my mother was a history major with emphasis in fine arts, I've seen many of the anecdotal persons from art and mythology named in this book, experienced their art and literature and stories in our own collections and the local library.  I've read their sad, sordid sense of moral turpitude, the decay of dynasties possessed of great art, luxury and wealth.<br /><br />Dorian's zeal to experience every pleasure on which he might lay his hands is reminiscent of the Ecclesiastes, though more lengthy and of less lucid outcomes.  Accused sometimes of misanthropy, Oscar Wilde is nonetheless a master of florid verbosity.  As once observed by author Ursula K. LeGuin, each sentence must dedicate it's reason for being, to an introduction of the next sentence.<br /><br />I might not have read all of this novel, except that I'm awed in admiration of the incredibly artful architecture of Wilde's sentences.  One simple sentence might introduce what literature teachers will scold their students about being a \\"run-on-sentence.\\"<br /><br />But Wilde philosophizes elaborate arguments in the heads of his characters.  There is nothing wrong analytically, or logically with attaching the cohesive phrases and clauses of two or more sides of an argument all together into a unified thought, even wielding a dozen commas to conjoin these articulated opposites.<br /><br />The style evokes memories of reading St. Paul in the King James English Bible, or the sublime constructions found in the tragedy [[ASIN:B002WPFH9K Ramona (Signet Classics)]] , by Helen Hunt Jackson.  I would never subject juvenile readers to the philosophy of Dorian Grey.  Even as an adult, I'm grateful to have experienced a healthy dose of Shakespeare, prior to microscopically examining the minutia of a deeply troubled mind preoccupied with male beauty.ce.\\"  But Wilde philosophizes elaborate arguments in the heads of his characters. 0765365278|4.0|I've read a fair bit of Brandon Sanderson's work, including his Mistborn Novels, Warbreaker and his collaboration with the late Robert Jordan (I say collaboration since Mr Jordan did leave a lot of material and direction for his books). It's fair to say that the quality of the writing has improved from series to series. The ideas and creativity of the worlds Mr. Sanderson invents are improving as well, with the world that this novel and series will take place in, Roshar, being one of the more original settings I've read in recent times. Don't look for cows, sheep, deer etc. The closest thing to this world that you might find is in the intertidal zone at your local beach, except substitute wind for waves as the defining element shaping the environment. It's interesting and quite original I would say. It's quite possible to get absorbed in studying the world itself, and the excellent art work included in the book helps the flavour a lot.  The magic system in this world, such as it is, is also quite interesting and original, and includes a fusion of technology which helps the flavour and believability.  That having been said, the three focus characters in the book vary considerably in their appeal, IMHO. Kaladin, the surgeon's son, who leaves a promising medical career to join the army to protect his younger brother is engaging. His personal character does undergo development as the story unfolds. You can't place him in any of the generic fantasy categories. for example, \\"heroic farmboy discovers he is the focus of the world's well-being\\", or \\"shining paladin who adheres to nobility even amidst the corruption of society\\". He's quite easy to like and empathise with, even if he does have power that renders him practically invincible, so long as his \\"mana battery\\" so to speak, if fully charged. Don't hesitate to empathise, get involved with and like this character. Unlike some of the major characters in some other works (I'm looking at you, George R.R. Martin), I doubt very much Kaladin is in any danger of dying midway through the series.  Dalinar, the uncle of the land of Alethkar's king, is easier to put into a generic category (see shining paladin, above). A man of strong principles and conviction, he is willing to sacrifice for the greater good. Even amidst his angst and the challenges he faces, you never doubt that he will rise to the occasion and do the right thing. He's easy to like, but maybe not so easy to believe, in such a ruthless and cut throat world.  Shallan, the third focus character, does have some ties to the main story, but her part is somewhat underwritten, probably to be more focused on in future novels. She's a nice enough girl, even with her impure motives, so I shall withold judgement of her until I've read at least 2 more books in the series. Since she is the source of some of the fine art work shown throughout the book though, I've no real complaints about her.  All in all, I would recommend this book to the majority of fantasy readers. It's easy to read and engaging. It didn't \\"rock my world\\" like Book 1 of the Wheel of Time, or Book 1 of a Song of Ice and Fire, but it is of better quality than most of the fantasy genre books I've read recently. I do suggest you pick it up if you enjoy a good read.

B004GHYCKW|5.0|English singer PJ Harvey released her new album \\"Let England Shake\\" in early 2011,. However, it was done so on a new label. It was her first project on Vagrant Records after a nearly 20 year association with Island Records. On this new album, she produces the album with her longtime collaborators John Parish (who she collaborated with on the album \\"A Man A Woman Walked By\\" in 2009), Flood (who produced two of Harvey's earlier albums- 1995's \\"To Bring You My Love\\" and 1998's \\"Is This Desire?\\") and Mick Harvey- who isn't related to PJ. On this new album, she decides to mellow things out and tell the story of her native homeland. A native of England, she retells the story of her country and the wars that endured that made it what it is in the present day.<br /><br />She starts the album off with the somber title track \\"Let England Shake\\", while leading into \\"The Last Living Rose\\"- a song sung from the perspective of a prisoner of war. On the percussive driven \\"The Glorious Land\\", it reflects on the numerous adults and children killed in the middle of battle in Afghanistan. The songs \\"The Words That Maketh Murder\\"  and \\"All And Everyone\\" talks about the horrors of war from the point of view from a soldier. \\"In The Dark Places\\" gives an account of the daily methods of the young English soldiers, while the people try to recover from the violence in their city on the song \\"Written On The Forehead\\". PJ closes the album with \\"The Colour Of The Earth\\", where a person remembers a friend who lost his life while in active duty. Overall, PJ Harvey's \\"Let England Shake\\" is a rather dark and melancholy record, but is a very captivating listen. It's noticeably subdued- even by PJ Harvey's standards- but works well with the emotion that is displayed throughout the album. In short, it's another great album from a great singer-songwriter.

One appetite-whetting comment by Rosen - on the main theme of the Adagio of the Trio in A-flat Hoboken #14 or Doblinger's #27 - \\"A melody of such uncomplicated beauty, it could be used at a funeral to be absolutely certain of a few tears.\\"  Rosen sadly notes - \\"It is odd to have to defend some of the greatest music ever written.  In any case the Haydn trios are doomed.  Only pianists will ever want to play them, and the modern piano recital is no place for them.\\"  It seemed to me (I'd like to think) that shortly after this book came out, later in the 70's, the Beaux Arts Trio took up a challenge and issued them nearly complete, as there seemed at the time to be no other effort to record them that was anywhere near comprehensive. I have no evidence that they ever read and were fired up by Rosen's chapter, and thus with any determination set out to refuse to allow this music's \\"doom\\" or anything. It's just one of those things that seems could have some truth and would be nice if it did.  I gave a rating to the 70's vinyls since I can't specifically review the actual new release.  I offered the comments on the old if it helps this discussion.  It was great music then, can't imagine it slipped for this release.

B0064NTZQ2|5.0|Very few films can capture the intensity that `Drive' is able to contain, and do so with such bridled passion for every frame.  What Nicolas Winding Refn does here is similar in scope to what David Fincher was able to achieve with last years `The Social Network'.  Every frame is beautifully composed to exact from us the precise emotion relevant to our understanding and appreciation of the film's structural progression.<br /><br />In other words, this is the directorial masterpiece of the year.<br /><br />But, `Drive' is far more than a mere moody masterwork.  A friend of mine mentioned that this is cinematic to the bone, and that is so true.  Every frame is a visual work of genius, so expressive in its ability to convey richness in tones with a mere shading of atmosphere.  The way that the film is perfectly edited (I mean, for a film so brisk in running time, it manages to fill it's frames with intense action AND detailed character development) suits the film's overall progression and remains instilled in the audience's mind long after the credits have finished rolling.  With muted moments (the dialog is far less important than the body language), `Drive' is a stunning cinematic achievement in a year composed and stunning cinematic achievements (seriously, this year is AMAZING).<br /><br />Ryan Gosling adds yet another knock out performance to his long list of intriguing and diverse characters.  Here he plays a driver (never named) who spends his days working in a garage for a man named Shannon who gets him in to do stunt work for movies and also, when needed, to drive getaway for crooks.  By chance, this driver meets a young mother in the same apartment building he is in.  Her name is Irene and her husband is currently serving time in prison.  They obviously have a connection but that connection is severed when her husband, Standard, is released from prison.  Standard owes some bad people some money but he is unable to pay it, and since the driver doesn't want to see something bad happen to Irene or her son, he offers to help Standard out.<br /><br />Big mistake.<br /><br />The second half of `Drive' shifts gears from brooding character study to intensity driven action flick, but those two worlds mesh marvelously thanks to Gosling's impenetrable stare and Winding Refn's superb handling of each scene's emotional dimensions.  The 80's inspired soundtrack coupled with the purposeful toying with time and speed (that elevator sequence is particularly magnificent) help establish `Drive' as an entity all its own.<br /><br />Yes, this is NOT what you think it'll be, but it is everything you could ask it to be.ne or her son, he offers to help Standard out. B0069UP2PI|5.0|`Contagion' is one of those films that continues to grow in my eyes as one of the best (in the very least, one of the most underrated) films of 2011.  Ever since first seeing it upon its release, it has only grown in esteem to me; a film that is truly inspired and original in its dissection of a global horror story.  From the film's claustrophobic cinematography to its sharply spliced editing to the perfectly timed performances that soak in the realism of every scene; `Contagion' feels like a documentary embellished with a director's visual approach.<br /><br />In `Contagion', a young woman named Beth contracts what she assumes is a cold, but that cold then spreads rapidly, killing many as it overtakes the globe.  From the vantage point of the doctors striving to find a cure, `Contagion' bypasses the clich&eacute;d approach taken by most viral films (that is, merely focusing on riots in the street and social and moral decline) but giving us a fresh and intriguing look at what happens `behind the scenes'.  Soderbergh and company develop a story that screams realism, from those working for the greater good as well as those focused on stardom and or financial benefits; and it never shies away from presenting the film's `heroes' as imperfect men.<br /><br />With an impressive cast (who nearly all deliver noteworthy performances), Soderbergh's `Contagion' is a magnificent thriller that sends the chills without every sacrificing the intelligence needed to make this film memorable.

Eternity is more acceptable than 'one time creation'.<br /><br />Here we have compressed explanation of history, discoveries like acceleration and how Universe may end (though this is much better presented in [[ASIN:0684865769 The Five Ages of the Universe: Inside the Physics of Eternity]] by Fred Adams).<br /><br />Krauss writes for laymen/masses, but content may be painful if this is somebody's first book about quantum/particles and concordance cosmology. This has been commented at large, but I will mention just one example: \\"..the energy of empty space cannot be physically infinite..\\"(p.71). If we have energy in space, that means space is not empty, because we all know that mass is a 'frozen' energy.<br /><br />The 'from nothing' idea in variety of forms, has been around since Kant. Krauss does not present anything revelatory. He just sensationalizes it.<br /> I am not convinced about \\"from nothing\\". We cannot produce something from nothing; the mass of produced particle must come from conversion of energy from some other form to this one. To make a massive particle such as a Z-boson we must cause a particle collision with high enough available energy. Similar statement can be found in ALL science books. The same doubt had prominent and influential John Archibald Wheeler. \\"How does something arise from nothing?\\", he asked about the existence of space and time (Princeton Physics News, 2006).<br />Casimir effect shows that empty space is not empty, but NOTHING so far has been manufactured or jumped out from between the plates during experiments in the lab.<br /><br />Krauss admits that nothing = 'almost nothing' (p.148) - fine with me, but it is obvious, that title has been manipulated to attract targeted cohorts of readers.<br />And more: can Krauss demonstrate he is right about 'nothing is unstable'? So how is Dr. Krauss?<br /><br />General conclusion: \\"A Universe from Nothing\\" is a very mediocre one among plethora of truly popular science books written by cosmology leaders (for example, written 12 years ago by John Barrow [[ASIN:0375726098 The Book of Nothing: Vacuums, Voids, and the Latest Ideas about the Origins of the Universe]] is largely superior, representing great depth in explainng 'vacuum's nothingness'). I also noticed that great astrophysicist Martin Rees (2011 Templeton Prize winner !!) endorsed the book. This stunned me, what a shame! Rees got the prize for tolerance andsupport of co-existence of religion and science!<br /><br />Krauss himself is a writer/speaker, but not the best. He is just a run of the mill, bored academic. He has not discovered anything important in cosmology. He does not have his own theory or model he could research. He is not Guth, Weinberg, Susskind, he is not Turok , neither he is Steinhardt , Penrose, Hawking or Vilenkin or Linde. He just goes around and sells well known, regurgitated, old idea, spiced up by attacks against people who dare to be spiritual. He goes after $$$ , that is all.<br /><br />In this book he galloped so fast, that he lost ability to control his knowledge.<br /><br />I always try to get the book written by scientist who has something new to propose, has discovered something new, made significant paradigm shift or lead a team of colleagues into successful project, etc. There are more names, like: John Barrow, Robert Kirshner, Maurizio Gasperini, George Smoot, Kep Thorne, Frank Wilczek, Leon Lederman. I live near the Perimeter Institute (Waterloo) where Lee Smolin and John Moffat work, and sometimes go there to attend public lectures (I have not seen Krauss there and I hope he will never come here).<br />All these people have achieved something - Krauss has nothing spectacular in his sleeve, nothing. History of cosmology will never mention him in the future.<br /><br />I keep [[ASIN:B000FZDKR0 Hiding in the Mirror: The Mysterious Allure of Extra Dimensions, from Plato to String Theory and Beyond]] on my shelf among other pop cosmo-science books. 014312093X|4.0|John LeCarre's Tinker Tailor Soldier Spy is as important historically as it is to the genre of espionage fiction. LeCarre' not only presented an alternative to Ian Fleming's fantasy spy world presented through James Bond, but also created a fictional world of duplicity and corruption eating away at institutions like a cancer that mirrored real life cases of double agents such as Kim Philby. The novel appeared in the perfect climate for lapsed faith in institutions. The Cold War was still raging, although cases such as Philby's raised questions about its success. It was published early in 1974; later that year, across the pond, Richard Nixon was forced into resignation by revelations brought out by the Watergate scandal. LeCarre's lexicon has also exerted a powerful influence on institutional corruption. The term `mole' has become almost as ubiquitous as `Catch-22'.  LeCarre's anti-Bond hero, George Smiley, has been (forcibly) retired from MI6 only to be called back into clandestine service by his government to follow clues indicating that a mole has been operating for the Soviets from within British intelligence. Smiley is not only an excellent spy; his deductive powers rank alongside master detectives such as Holmes and Poirot. He follows leads and false leads down dizzying mazes that would tax the most astute minds until he narrows his focus to the point where he possesses certainty.  The maze that extends outward in multiple directions from Smiley's initial covert assignment possesses the verisimilitude of life and is just as confusing, frustrating and ambiguous. One would need a large wall-sized diagram of the interlocking characters positioned in their overt roles with alternate images for their covert characters to understand everything that happens in this novel. The good news is that all the confusion does end with a powerful payoff that proves worthy of the journey.  LeCarre', through Smiley, the master spy that is as loyal to his agency and his governmentas one could find, expresses the ambivalence that appears inevitable for anyone who finally sees the broad picture that portrays spies as merely players in a global game. One's allegiance is secondary to one's kinship with a fellow practitioner. Smiley and Karla are almost mirror images of each other; Smiley gives the cigarette lighter given to him by his wife to Karla to solidify that presumptive bond. By the conclusion of the novel, when Smiley lays the trap for the mole and confirms his identity as a friend and colleague, he understands his friend's reasons and, while he falls short of re-evaluating his own loyalty, he sees with greater clarity the weaknesses in the foundation of the bureaucracy he has spent all his adult life serving.

014312093X|4.0|John Le Carre's \\"Tinker, Tailor, Soldier Spy\\" was first publishe in 1974. The title is taken from a nursery rhyme and is used by Le Carre to refer to the chief suspects of being spies for the Soviets. The novel lept to the top of the heap of novels in the international espionage genre. The book was made into a fine BBC series and is now a major motion picture. Though the action takes place in the cold war 1970s it is stll a gripping read in the tradition of Eric Ambler and Graham Greee.<br />  The Plot is very complicated demanding the reader's full attention! The antihero is world weary George Smiley who has been fired from the \\"Circus.\\" (the British secret service). Smiley is flabby, world weary, old and his wife Ann has left him for another chap at the office.<br />  Smiley is surprisingly asked to root out a \\"mole\\" in British intelligence. A mole is an enemy spy who is able to burrow into the inner circle of a belligerent's spy service.. The novel consists of Smiley going over old files and interviewing many persons who were involved n the case of Jim Ellis. Ellis was a spy who was captured in Czech. by the Soviets in the 1970s.<br />  What makes the book stand out above others in the overcrowded spy genre?<br />a. Le Carre is a master of complex plots. British and secret service slang and jargon can make the book read slowly for Yankee eyes.<br />b. Le Carre has so many characters in his tales that it is hard to remember who is who and who is speaking.<br />c. Do not expect wild chases and sex escapades a la James Bond in LeCarre' somber tales. The author is an intellectual who writes for adults.<br />  Le Carre's books are classics which, despite their flaws, hold up well and are worth reading.

B005C7SXMS|5.0|Given the fact that Submarine is prefaced by an introduction claiming that it is \\"an important film, watch it with respect,\\" I had my doubts going in. Many indie films find themselves to be far more important than they actually are. The \\"produced by Ben Stiller\\" label that can be found on the DVD cover and in the opening credits wasn't a good sign either. Films that advertise a famous person who didn't direct or star in it (though Stiller has a very minor cameo), generally do so because they don't have much else going for them. But once Submarine got started, I was blown away by the film's undeniable charm.  Submarine is told from the perspective of Oliver Tate, a young fifteen-year-old bent on saving his parents relationship and losing his virginity. Young Oliver is a genius and carries the typical pretentiousness that you might expect, but his obliviousness is countered by his vulnerability. Oliver isn't the kind of guy you would like if you went to school with him, but you can't help but feel sympathy for him as you watch him struggle with situations that he can't control.  Coming of age movies have been done for decades, but it's still incredibly satisfying when one's done properly. He struggles with girls as any fifteen year old would, but he doesn't turn to outlandish stunts to get his girlfriend Jordana, who dumped in after he struggled to offer her comfort while her mother battled cancer, back. He doesn't stand outside her window blasting Peter Gabriel from a boom box and he doesn't deliver any one-liners. His approach is rather normal. This might be unappealing to some who look to movies as an escape from reality, but indie fans will gobble this movie up.  Submarine is an incredibly clever movie, but it's powered by humor that one might find in real life. It's advertised as a biopic and it lives up to that, even if the bio is about someone that isn't famous at all. You get to know Oliver and you get to care about him. It goes beyond the struggles of a too smart for his own good teenager searching to get laid, it's a tale of humanity that anyone can relate to. You wouldn't expect a movie about a fifteen-year-old Welsh boy to carry universal appeal, but you'd be surprised.  If you like indie movies, give Submarine a shot. I still don't think it's an \\"important film\\" as it may claim to be, but it's certainly one of the most charming movies I've seen in a long time. The art of the teen film was perfected in the 80s and many films have failed to reinvent the wheel. Submarine doesn't come from the same mold as The Breakfast Club or Sixteen Candles, but it does show the vulnerability that teenage years inevitably usher in. Few films enter that territory for any other reason that to get a few laughs. While I don't mean to put those films down, I appreciated Submarine's effort to accurately portray the realization of helplessness that many teenagers experience.

B000001FGG|3.0|Hey it took me long enough to finally hear Wheels of Fire considering the enormous amount of praise I've been hearing about, but the wait is now over and it turns out I enjoy about 75% of the album.  Basically you have one half devoted to the studio side of Cream, and their live jammy side is captured on the second disc.  Despite my 3-star rating, it's actually because \\"Toad\\" and \\"Traintime\\" are weak and unnecessary to me, and that clocks in at around 24 minutes of music.  Let's start with the live material, shall we?  \\"Spoonful\\" doesn't hit the mark with the vocals like in the studio version because this version lacks punch for whatever reason, however that's the ONLY thing lacking because \\"Spoonful\\" makes up for that with a really lengthy and highly enjoyable guitar jam.  It's not much different from the jams I've heard Cream attempt such as on Live Cream for example, and in fact speaking of Live Cream, the jam from \\"N.S.U\\" and the one on this version of \\"Spoonful\\" definitely strike me as being performed in an identical way.  Maybe not the same note for note, but close enough.  However, the good news is that it's impossible to ignore that much of it is in fact quite melodic, which probably explains why everyone loves it so much.  \\"Toad\\" starts off with a smoky barroom-like guitar solo before quickly switching into an extended 14-minute drum solo.  Normally drum solos are one of the LAST things I want to hear, but you know, this one actually has its share of memorable moments.  Right around the 10-minute mark especially (when the drums resemble African tribal dancing) is probably my favorite part.  Still, a good 5 minutes needs to be trimmed, if not more (such as right around the 11-minute mark- THIS is when it starts to become overkill and should have ended right there).  \\"Crossroads\\" is a popular song for a reason (this version especially).  It takes the heavy blues rock formula and makes it super catchy.  Its appeal is hard to explain too since the entire song is based around a guitar riff that's honestly not THAT great.  \\"Traintime\\" is a bluesy song with lots of harmonica and drums, but something feels slightly off about it.  The way the drums plod along is sort of uninteresting, the harmonica repeats the same few notes too many times, and the vocals feel rushed.  I don't know, I can't get into this one.  As for the studio stuff, THIS is where I'm blown away (well, \\"Spoonful\\" blows me away too, I admit).  \\"White Room\\" is the best song the band ever made.  Everything about the vocal melody, to the fierce drum work, to the guitar solo at the end, to the dramatic heart-breaking line \\"I'll wait in this place where the sun never shines, wait in the dark where the shadows run from themselves\\" is a MAGNIFICENT song.  The atmosphere always reminded me of being stuck in a rundown prison somewhere that's extremely hot.  \\"Politician\\" has a super heavy guitar riff that probably influenced Black Sabbath.  I say \\"probably\\" because I know Bill Ward was a fan of Cream at least- not sure about the other band members.  \\"Sitting On Top of the World\\" has a fantastic vocal melody.  It also has a guitar riff that's pretty darn heavy by late 60's standards, and it holds up amazingly well.  \\"Passing the Time\\" has a gentle flower power-like vocal melody that's a pretty drastic departure from the rest of the album.  Reminds me of the Zombies or Love without the refined songwriting approach to make it really excel.  Still, it's enjoyable.  \\"As You Said\\" is another experiment, this one being an unusual psychedelic tune that has elements of the Beatles classic \\"The Inner Light\\" as far as spooky atmosphere is concerned.  The way the guitar plods along is another strange element to the songs, well, strange nature.  Not sure what to think about the verse melody though.  It's good enough I suppose.  \\"Pressed Rat and Warthog\\" contains one of my favorite verse melodies, even though it's questionable whether it's really being SUNG or not.  The melodic background arrangements really enhance the lyrics and atmosphere.  \\"Those Were the Days\\" is *awesome* too!  The lyrics about Atlantis and the soaring/angelic way the vocals lead into the catchy drum work is a thing of brilliance.  \\"Born Under a Bad Sign\\" is something I've always been skeptical about given my less than spectacular luck.  Quality vocal work and lyrics.  \\"Deserted Cities of the Heart\\" is one of those songs that REALLY reminds me of childhood memories.  Not sure why.  The vocal delivery just sails and plays with my emotions exactly the right way to give me feelings of nostalgia and daydreaming adventures.  Overall, the studio stuff is extremely fantastic.  The live stuff?  Well it's an acquired taste to be honest, and besides \\"Spoonful\\" the rest of it fails to make an impression on me, hence the 3.5-star rating.

B000002P2W|4.0|Who By Numbers is an album I find to be eclectic within the Who's previously powerful outputs of Quadrophenia, Who's Next and Tommy and is much more simpler, yet all the more a strong effort from the group and on Townshend's behalf; and why shouldn't it be a little more simpler? It is although, seemingly, as most of the lyrics can be interpreted as addressing the problems of getting older and the reprecussions of being a celebrity, if one digs through certain lyrical references.  By this point, Townshend and the Who were in order for a break at the end of supporting this effort, but the material stands up today as quite an achievement. Slip Kid begins with a 6/8 time signature and the band crash in accordingly with a track that has all of the elements of the Who firing off on the essential cylinders, backed by a lyrical reference of \\"there's no easy way to be free\\", while drawing the parallels of a kid at 13 and the older man at 63. However Much I Booze tells another tale of desperation here in which the reference is \\"there ain't no way out\\". Apparently, Pete was reflecting on his life in this time period with very dim and tired eyes it seems. Imagine a Man is bittersweet in its delivery and its title starkingly accords to the music that has been arranged as its extremely sparse, melancholy, yet brave also.  Squeeze Box is a simple number which features Pete taking a banjo solo as opposed to the standard electric guitar break! And is a jigging, bouncy type of tune that hit the top twenty chart upon its release and became a live favorite on the tour. Blue, Red and Grey features Townshend on vocal and plays a ukulele accompanied by a horn arrangement, that offers a brighter view in this fun little number. John Entwistle offers up Success Story, a tight little rocker which references a semi-autobiography of the band's life, tongue in cheek, mind you. Dreaming From the Waist and In a Hand or a Face join Slip Kid and Success Story in the more rocking side of things, while addressing a more cynical, cryptic at times, look upon life and all of its peaks and valleys one has to endure to achieve some sort of understanding to it all. Pretty tough stuff, there! Performance wise, Moon was still at the peak of his powers before faltering off with the impending haze of alcohol that would ultimately, lead to his undoing. Entwistle's bass playing is as powerful, prolific and propelling as ever as he adds the depth to numbers like Slip Kid, In a Hand Or a Face, Dreaming from the Waist and However Much I Booze with amazing dexterity. Daltrey's vocals are robust with energy and a defiant sense of conquer despite the lyrics Pete had written for the record, a problem that would arise on the follow-up Who Are You set. Still, though, Daltrey's voice has always been a welcomed power and style that defines 1/4 the sound the Who.  Who By Numbers is more of a personal and psychological update within Townshend and the Who camp that succeeds well in its intents as a mid-life album that sometimes can be depressing, yet all the more interesting as well. 4 stars

B000002P6R|4.0|After the death of Keith Moon, the Who soldiered on and recruited former Small Faces/Faces drummer Kenny Jones as Moon's replacement and continued performing live up until late '82 before suspending band activity for an indefinite hiatus. Choosing famed producer Bill Szymczyk(Joe Walsh, the Eagles, Elvin Bishop), the Who worked on Face Dances over a 5 month period at Odyssey Studios in London and despite some critical objections, the album pushed its way into the top 5 and produce a top 20 single in You Better, You Bet, which consolidated the Who's updated sound for the 1980s. After the release of Townshend's Empty Glass and success of the single Let My Love Open the Door from the previous year, many claimed that Face Dances really didn't sound very Who-ish and was merely an extension of Townshend's solo work with the members of the Who as back-up musicians. This is simply not true to my ears, as I hear a revitalized band that has gained a second momentum to survive, at least for the few more years that remained for itself, and does so accordingly.  Pop sensibility and a somewhat New wave influence permeates throughout Townshend and the group's collective sound which culminates in a forward moving fashion with tracks like You Better, You Bet, Don't Let Go the Coat and Daily Records displaying a totally revamped and modified sound for the band. Gone, of course, is the maniacal drumming of Moon in exchange for the more contemporary, but just as powerful, drumming skills of Jones and I think he does a credible job throughout the recording. Entwistle weighs in with the heavier tracks on the record in the ripping Quiet One, where he sings lead, and the short, sharp, snappiness of You rendered by a powerful vocal from Daltrey.  Townshend offers a variatable sense of nuances within his songwriting and it comes to the fore on tracks like Cache Cache, Did You Steal My Money(which utilizes stereo mixing to a high advantage) and Daily Records which are compelling numbers and show a significant amount of growth within Pete's songwriting skills. How Can You Do It Alone seems a bit banal at times and slightly pedestrian in its delivery, but still maintains a fresh poppy feel within itself. John \\"Rabbit\\" Bundrick adds a fifth element with his keyboard contributions all over this record and songs that benefit greatly from his presence are You Better, You Bet, Daily Records, Did You Steal My Money and the record's closer Another Tricky Day, which is one of the more consistently powerful numbers on offer here. Even though the Who were entering the final stages of their 18 year career, Face Dances proves that the band could, in fact, remain relevant to an ever changing genre of music and do it with the strength and wisdom of a well weathered and refined rock outfit. 4 stars

B000059T1G|3.0|Hold on before anyone might misunderstand me on my review title: I like Last Exit for what it offers, even though Mason only appears on two cuts, not to mention the fact that Traffic had ceased by the time this was released, anyways; it is an interesting footnote on the first phase of the group and features some strong tracks in Mason's Just For You(a solo B-side that was augmented by the rest of the band on this version) which contains the typical Mr. Fantasy vibe that was displayed on that debut with a folky/psychedelic tinge in its songcraft and the  exceptional flute playing of Chris Wood. Winwood's Shanghai Noodle Factory recalls a bit of Who Knows What Tomorrow May Bring from the self-titled second album in its melody and impact, with further accompaniment from Wood on the flute. Something's Got a Hold of My Toe is a cutting-room instrumental that was picked to fill the album, really; despite some interesting guitar licks from Mason and an upbeat jam quality, its shoehorned in the tracklist.  Withering Tree is a piano driven Winwood cut that offers a chilly ambience to its melody arrangement, once again complimented by the great flute playing of Chris Wood. Medicated Goo is a grooving rocker that would later open '71's Welcome to the Canteen and provides some exceptional guitar playing from the vastly talented Winwood and the solid drumming of Jim Capaldi. Feelin Good and Blind Man are taken from a show at the Fillmore West when Traffic had been finishing up gigs without Mason and are strong covers that come across competently and with confidence; though one misses the fourth element, at times of a guitar, as these numbers are essentially sax and organ fueled workouts to compensate for that void, the trio of Winwood, Capaldi and Wood pull it off well. The construction of this album is really more for serious Traffic fans, rather than casual ones, despite the uneveness and lack of cohesion here. 3 and a half stars.

B000059T1E|4.0|Although this finished album began life as a solo effort from Steve Winwood, John Barleycorn Must Die is an amazing kickstart for phase II of Traffic, along with its successor Low Spark of Hi-Heeled Boys, making for an era that rivals the previous incarnation of musical interest and merit. Winwood worked with Guy Stevens whom was set to produce Winwood's debut solo album(working title Mad Shadows, the title eventually used by Guy's protege's Mott the Hoople) but after a brief time, the sessions were scrapped and as Winwood's former colleagues in Traffic(Jim Capaldi and Chris Wood) returned to the fold, Steve elected to produce the record himself along with help from Island label owner Chris Blackwell. The songs that did emerge from the Winwood/Stevens sessions, however showed off his virtuosity and sense of masterful songwriting in Stranger to Himself(All instruments played by Steve) and Every Mothers Son, with help from Capaldi on drums. Included in the remaster edition is Sittin Here Thinkin of My Love, an outtake from the same Stevens sessions, although its easy to see why it was left on the cutting-room floor, as its a bit subpar lacking in overall substance.  Largely a collaboration between Capaldi and Winwood in songcrafts, John Barleycorn is a record wherein Steve Winwood takes the reins on this phase of the group and is duly honorable in furthering his talents as a songwriter and musician. the opening salvo of Glad is a piano/sax driven track that has elements of soul and jazz interlaid, leading into the more darker aspects of Freedom Rider which takes its cues from Glad's musical flavor and adds more depth within the duration of this number. Empty Pages is domineered by Winwood's organ and electric piano arrangement and Capaldi's tightly knit kit beats which makes for a delightful listen. The title track is rendered an acoustic folk arrangement with the ominous flute of Chris Wood and great vocal harmonies from Winwood and Capaldi, culminating in a strong track and centerpiece for the record. Phase 2 of Traffic was actually a much needed impetus for the gifted trio of players, that may have been a bit overshadowed by their former partner in Dave Mason from a few years past and emerge triumphant in their first full outing on vinyl with some marvelous results. 4 solid stars

B00006399Z|5.0|Recorded in the summer of '71, Welcome to the Canteen temporarily reunites Dave Mason with the core trio of Steve Winwood, Jim Capaldi and Chris Wood backed by the dynamic rhythm section of Rick Grech(Bass) and Jim Gordon(drums) culminating in a live set that is powerful, energetic and simply extraordinary. Culled from performances at Croydon's Fairfield Hall and a benefit concert in London for the Oz satirical publication(at the time, was under scruntiny for alleged obscenity under lawful grounds), although the record is a mere 6 tracks, the performances contained herein are hot and smoking and provide further evidence of the resurge in Traffic's reconstruction as a functional live entity. Opening with the groovin' rocker, Medicated Goo, Winwood and Capaldi harmonize brilliantly on this take and Mason provides some searing guitar work interlaid to build up the excitement of this killer song. Mason's \\"unplugged\\" Sad and Deep as You provides a moving and stirring number that has depth and power rendered with the percussion talents of Rebop Kwakuu Baah and Mason's tasteful acoustic guitar backed by the inimitable flute playing of Chris Wood.  40,000 Headmen continues the \\"unplugged\\" vibe here and is a faithful live rendition taken from the band's 2nd self-titled release, with a powerful vocal from Winwood. Shouldn't Have Took More than You Gave is another showcase for Dave Mason featuring his soulful vocal reading and exceptional wah-wah guitar arrangement, carried by Winwood's searing organ flourishes. The real meat of Canteen is justified by the album's closers, a dynamite and definitive version of Dear Mr. Fantasy(which features a guitar workout between Mason and Winwood) and a smoldering cover of Winwood's early Spencer Davis hit Gimme Some Lovin which boogies and rocks its way along quite compellingly. All told, Canteen is a brief, yet extraordinary moment in time, that is another piece of history signifying Traffic's importance within late 60's/early 70's bridgebetween pop/rock tendencies and adding a flair of true musical improvisation to the genre. Which is a certain quality that I feel Traffic had achieved within their inception and separated them from their contemporaries of the time period. Essential and Classic. 5 stars

B000002P2V|4.0|Although this album would prove to be Keith Moon's swan song with the band, Townshend's songwriting transcends from the previous Who By Numbers set and settles in more nicely as a matured and accomplished release. Lots of problems had been encountered once the band began recording this album in late Oct. of 1977, after an extended break taken by the band members, and it was Keith Moon whose time away had been proven most detrimental to his musicanship. Suffering from the excessive behaviors he had subjected to himself, in terms of alcohol use, coupled with an excessive weight gain, had done damage to Moon's health and ultimately his drumming capabilities. Townshend had begun experiencing gradual hearing loss, damaged due in part, to the agressively loud performances over a number of years and began to utilize special headsets in the studio to help aid his acute hearing handicap.  Famed engineer Glyn Johns, whom had been involved in the reconstruction of Who's Next and their last studio recording By Numbers, was selected to work with the band in the shaping of the record. Attempting a live feel would soon prove to be a disasterous exercise and after utilizing different studios and another engineer in Jon Astley, Who Are You remains a pieced together affair that suprisingly turns out rather nicely given the overlaborous care it required to get finished. New Song opens the record and right away, there is a sense of rejuvination and an update within the group's overall sound that is fresh and contemporary in its feel. Moon's drumming seems a bit toned down from albums past, and it is a bit sterile, at times, but also still provides the needed heartbeat and backbone to the ensemble. The use of synthesizers is very prominent on this release and also adds another texture of color and sound to enhance tracks like Sister Disco, Entwistle's 905 and of course, the album's title track.  Music Must Change utilizes footsteps which provide the desired percussion effect within thesongs quirky 6/8 time signature that Moon failed to successfully nail from previous takes. At times Daltrey's vocal delivery on this number is a little over the top esp. in the bridge of the track, but overall, exudes a thoroughly realized original Townshend number. Trick of the Light is another Entwistle original that is mid-paced and benefits from his heavily distorted 8 string Alembic bass that provides the main fused melody to this strong Who number, utilizing, again synthesizer flourishes to add texture and color. Guitar and Pen is perhaps, a bit outside of the box of typical Who songs and hints at the future sound of the band post-Moon on Face Dances, for example, which again is innovative for its time and adds a freshness that is most welcomed. Love is Coming Down is a ballad-type track that has a pleasant feel to it, but is also derivative and slightly generic in its subtleness. Sister Disco is a tuneful assault and a retort to the changing trends of pop music, in all its forms, and becomes another important track within the growth of the Who and their musical palette.  The title track, of course, is the most revered and popular song taken from this release and remains a legendary classic song which is quite labored in its arrangement and shows off Moon's reckless drumming style one more shining time. The remaster features two unreleased tracks sung by Townshend, the ballad-like No Road Romance and Empty Glass(later appearing on the future album of the same name) which features interesting bass lines from Entwistle and solid drumming from Moon; providing a neat demo take on this innovative and quirky tune. The last 3 bonus songs are different mixes of Guitar and Pen, Love is Comin Down and the \\"lost verse\\" version of Who Are You which also features further expletives that were partially disguised for commercial acceptance. In Closing, Who Are You is, in most respects, the last true Who release collectively, and unfortunately, the last to feature Moon the Loon. Their future releases with Kenny Jones were noble, in particular Face Dances, but Who Are You is really the final statement from the original lineup. 4 solid stars

B000002KOY|5.0|Sadly, Danny Whitten, whom was the main focus of Crazy Horse(previously The Rockets) died a couple of years after this stunning debut album and it serves as a testament as to why Neil Young had chosen the core of the Horse(Billy Talbot-Bass, Whitten-Guitar/vocals and Ralph Molina-Drums) to be his permanent backing group when he wasn't being preoccupied with the parallel of performing with Crosby, Stills & Nash. Accompanied by ace musician/arranger Jack Nitzsche and a very young Nils Lofgren, Crazy Horse is a lost gem within early 70's California rock and roll. Opening with Gone Dead Train(written by Jack and Russ Titelman) the sound is of Topanga Canyon with a touch of country thrown in for good measure and Whitten's vocals are typically of a high-lonesome cracked twange in his delivery. Neil's presence is evident on the writing credits of two numbers: Dance, Dance, Dance, which continues the country-rock vibe replete with a fiddle by Gib Gilbeau; and co-written with Whitten, Downtown that has a leaning rock/pop vibe about it and would feature again in live form on Young's Tonight's the Night album done in tribute to Whitten.  Nils Lofgren contributes 2 tracks in the forms of the Stones-esque Beggars Day, that has a subtle, yet, growing quality in its chord progression and darker melody tone, while Nobody is a tight shuffle that contains a similar approach in its arrangement, albeit more upbeat in the chorus, while the verse lays back. Nevertheless, Whitten and Lofgren work wonderfully together on these fine Lofgren originals and it further adds more balance to the record. Whitten's Look at All the Things is ballad-like and shows the vulnerability, but power in Whitten's singing, while Dirty, Dirty is a simple R&B blues workout and it jams pretty dirty, indeed! I Don't Want to Talk About It would later find success from Rod Stewart's version, but the original take here is simple, sparse and heartachingly beautiful; truly a real glimpse of Whitten's talents. I'll GetBy is a lazy, yet upbeat track that features fine, fine vocal harmonies and a carefree, healing feel musically. Carolay is another gem worth of mention as far as vocals, ensemble and performance goes; written again by Russ Titelman and Jack Nitzsche, this track is one of my favorite numbers on this set and should've been a single if it wasn't chosen already.  The album closes with a Nitzsche tune, Crow Jane Lady, sung by Jack and features Ry Cooder on slide guitar, most prominently; Ry had also guested on I Don't Want To Talk About It and Dirty, Dirty as well giving a little bit of a boost in the guitar dept. as Whitten's heroin abuse had affected the sessions. Despite this, Crazy Horse's debut remains the band's best recording out of its scattered output over the years and has stood the test of time. Highly Recommended to fans of early 70's California Rock. 5 BIG Stars....

The classic works demonstrating this principle have been [[ASIN:0452284236 Nineteen Eighty-Four]], [[ASIN:0060850523 Brave New World]], and [[ASIN:0451228146 One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich]]. The fall of the Berlin Wall and the resulting demise of the Cold War have left only a few Stalinist states.Many of the so-called Communist countries today have thriving and open free-market economies to which the one-party rule of those states actually promotes capitalist ideals.<br /><br />The biggest exception to this trend is North Korea, where The Orphan Master's Son is set. 145162445X|5.0|Lawrence Krauss has done everyone a great service by writing this book. Krauss, in \\"A Universe from Nothing,\\" explains our current picture of the cosmos: where it came from and where it is going. Although he covers a lot of ground, the book is fairly simple to read, though if you haven't read anything on cosmology, this book may be a bit daunting. If this is your first book on cosmology/physics, I'd recommend reading [[ASIN:0375708111 The Elegant Universe: Superstrings, Hidden Dimensions, and the Quest for the Ultimate Theory]] first, so you can get an idea for the general concepts.  My favorite section of the book is when Krauss explains the existence of virtual particles. Virtual particles, which are particles that don't actually exist, contribute energy, and therefore mass, to particles. The mass comes about from the Heisenberg Uncertainty Principle. There are two equations, but the one that we are interested in says any uncertainty regarding the amount of energy possessed by a particle and time (of duration) of that possession has to be equal or greater than Planck's Constant (h/2*pi). Shorter the time of possession is more energy these particles can possess and don't forget that according to Einstein (E=mc^2) energy is also mass. Krauss then extends this to empty space itself, which means empty space can have mass, and via general relativity, contribute a negative energy to space, causing it to expand (and explanation for dark energy?).  Some people have said that Krauss is \\"arrogant,\\" claiming that he has \\"all the answers.\\" This is far from the truth. As is apparent throughout the book, Krauss lets the reader know when things are speculations or uncertain. That's what made this a great read: even though we know a lot about the universe, there are many things we don't know, and that's something beautiful. It gives us something to ponder and search for the answers.  One thing that could be improved is possibly explaining the concepts a bit more (as described above) and including the actual equations so we can get a feel of what's going on. Even though Krauss says what the equations are, he writes them out, which could be hard to keep track of if you don't write it down.  For anyone interested in what we currently know about the cosmos, and what we do not, I highly recommend Krauss' book.

0439023521|5.0|You're probably wondering why I rated this book five stars given the title of my review.  Well, this first book in the Hunger Games Trilogy definitely deserves such a high rating, but I think readers should be warned about how the story progresses in the other two installments.  I found the first book to be truly an engaging and riveting read about Katniss Everdeen, one of twenty-four contestants fighting to the death in a politically-motivated and televised event orchestrated by the Capitol. The Capitol is seen as the ruler that forces a boy and a girl from twelve districts to participate in the Hunger Games each year to remind the people in the districts that they are at the Capitol's mercy.  Suzanne Collins delivers such an intense emotional experience that even when I took breaks from reading, I found myself thinking about the characters, the story, and even my own life.  The Hunger Games is not a sappy teenage novel in any stretch of the imagination-it is so much more, too complicated to sum up in this small review.  Katniss' action at the end of the first installment triggers a rebellion against the Capitol, which left me with a sense of hope that the district people would finally be able to fight against all the injustices brought on by the Capitol and succeed, but I was wrong. No one ever truly succeeds.  After the rebellion ensues, a war is triggered. The second book deals with the rebellion, the third with the war.  If you think the first installment carried with it a dark and depressing tone, well, you haven't even experienced the tip of the iceberg.  Almost all characters you grow to love meet horrible deaths. Katniss' family and friends aren't spared either. They are tortured and killed in agonizing detail. The strong heroine you meet in the opening chapters of The Hunger Games wittles away into a tormented soul, plagued by nightmares and haunting images of her friends and family. Her memories destroy her, and leave her a hollow shell.  She even loses her best friend; the horrors of the war, not death, driving them apart. This was so sad and gut-wrenching to me. Many readers argue that Collins betrays the true nature of this character for the sake of the ending, and I agree.  I loved the first half of Catching Fire, the second installment, as much as the first book until Katniss and Peeta are forced to endure another round of the games. This part signaled to me that there would be more darkness ahead rather than light.  Then, in the third installment, Mockingay, everything finally unravels and falls apart completely. The reader is left with no sense of hope, and it left me depressed. To be honest, I read a little over a third of Mockingjay before I decided that I wouldn't force myself to continue such a dreadful story. I skipped to the Epilogue just to get some closure, but there was none to be had.  Part of me wishes I would have never picked up this Trilogy, another part of me wishes I would have stopped after the first book and remembered the characters the way they were. Strong and full of hope.
